# Kyrgyz Republic

## Summary Assessment

Kyrgyz Republic is a Lower middle income economy in Europe & Central Asia, with the latest observation in 2022. The external-liquidity screen records a vulnerability score of 40.0 and a moderate status. The latest reserve position is US$2.8 billion, providing 3.2 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 2.88x. The current account shows a deficit of 42.7% of GDP, equal to -US$5.2 billion. The model-estimated baseline liquidity gap is 49.5% of GDP, or US$6.0 billion. The assessment is driven by the interaction of reserve coverage, short-term external debt exposure, and the current-account position.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2022, reported reserves were US$2.8 billion, compared with annual imports of US$10.6 billion and GDP of US$12.1 billion. Import cover stood at 3.2 months. Under the scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 50.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$972.4 million, and reserves covered that stock by 2.88x. The short-term-debt coverage component also does not add to the composite risk score in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$6.0 billion, or 49.5% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 42.7% of GDP, equivalent to -US$5.2 billion. The deficit is the main flow-side pressure in the dashboard, indicating a need for net external financing on the current-account measure. The current-account component carries a risk score of 100.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$2.8 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$5.2 billion. Imports of US$10.6 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$972.4 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. The latest reading should therefore be interpreted as a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage.

## Trend Interpretation

Over 2015-2022, the available indicators show how the liquidity position has evolved across reserve coverage, external flows, and the model-based liquidity gap. Import cover declined from 4.2 to 3.2 months, compared with a range of 3.2-8.3 months over the period. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt declined from 5.0x to 2.9x, while the observed range was 2.9x-6.1x. The current-account balance weakened from -15.8 percent to -42.7 percent of GDP, with observations ranging from -42.7 percent to 4.5 percent of GDP. This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap increased from 13.3 percent to 49.5 percent of GDP, against a range of -15.3 percent to 49.5 percent of GDP, leaving the liquidity cushion less comfortable than at the start of the period. On balance, the latest readings suggest more limited liquidity space and warrant closer monitoring of reserve buffers and external financing needs.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 40.0, with a moderate status and a vulnerability rank of 25 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is moderate vulnerability, placing the country between the low-risk group and the most pressured cases in the sample. The component scores are 50.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 100.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, which are outside the scope of this dashboard. The composite result is useful for cross-country screening, but the component indicators remain essential for identifying the source of vulnerability.

From a surveillance perspective, the ranking should be treated as a screening signal rather than a substitute for country-specific judgment. The component indicators remain more informative than the headline score: import cover captures reserve liquidity against trade needs, the short-term-debt ratio captures rollover exposure, and the baseline liquidity gap indicates whether usable reserves exceed the model's near-term liquidity requirement.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2015 and ends in 2022; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The latest observation is 2022, so the public-data indicators are incomplete for assessing developments after that year. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. Accordingly, the note should be read as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign-risk or balance-of-payments assessment.

<!-- COUNTRY: MAR | Morocco -->

# Morocco

## Summary Assessment

Morocco is a Lower middle income economy in Middle East, North Africa, Afghanistan & Pakistan, with the latest observation in 2024. The external-liquidity screen records a vulnerability score of 0.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$37.1 billion, providing 5.5 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 4.95x. The current account shows a deficit of 1.2% of GDP, equal to -US$1.9 billion. The model-estimated baseline liquidity gap is -4.7% of GDP, or -US$7.6 billion. The assessment is driven by the interaction of reserve coverage, short-term external debt exposure, and the current-account position.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$37.1 billion, compared with annual imports of US$80.6 billion and GDP of US$160.6 billion. Import cover stood at 5.5 months. Under the scoring framework, this import-cover position does not add to the composite risk score. Short-term external debt was US$7.5 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 4.95x. The short-term-debt coverage component also does not add to the composite risk score in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at -US$7.6 billion, or -4.7% of GDP, which indicates a reserve cushion under the packet's baseline liquidity metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 1.2% of GDP, equivalent to -US$1.9 billion. The deficit is the main flow-side pressure in the dashboard, indicating a need for net external financing on the current-account measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$37.1 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$1.9 billion. Imports of US$80.6 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$7.5 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The negative baseline gap indicates that reserves exceed the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. The latest reading should therefore be interpreted as a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage.

## Trend Interpretation

Over 2017-2024, the available indicators show how the liquidity position has evolved across reserve coverage, external flows, and the model-based liquidity gap. Import cover declined from 6.4 to 5.5 months, compared with a range of 5.3-9.4 months over the period. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt increased from 3.5x to 5.0x, while the observed range was 3.0x-5.0x. The current-account balance improved from -3.1 percent to -1.2 percent of GDP, with observations ranging from -4.9 percent to -1.0 percent of GDP. This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap narrowed from -2.4 percent to -4.7 percent of GDP, against a range of -12.3 percent to 2.4 percent of GDP, indicating some easing in the model-based liquidity gap. On balance, the latest position remains supported by reserve buffers, although the direction of individual indicators should continue to be monitored.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 0.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 86 out of 99 countries. The low classification indicates that the composite screen does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, which are outside the scope of this dashboard. The composite result is useful for cross-country screening, but the component indicators remain essential for identifying the source of vulnerability.

From a surveillance perspective, the ranking should be treated as a screening signal rather than a substitute for country-specific judgment. The component indicators remain more informative than the headline score: import cover captures reserve liquidity against trade needs, the short-term-debt ratio captures rollover exposure, and the baseline liquidity gap indicates whether usable reserves exceed the model's near-term liquidity requirement.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. Accordingly, the note should be read as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign-risk or balance-of-payments assessment.

The latest reading should therefore be interpreted as a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage.

## Trend Interpretation

Over 2017-2024, the available indicators show how the liquidity position has evolved across reserve coverage, external flows, and the model-based liquidity gap. Import cover remained broadly stable from 3.9 to 3.8 months, compared with a range of 3.7-8.8 months over the period. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt increased from 4.1x to 7.4x, while the observed range was 4.1x-9.7x. The current-account balance was broadly unchanged from -15.8 percent to -17.0 percent of GDP, with observations ranging from -26.2 percent to -9.3 percent of GDP. This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap was broadly unchanged from 16.2 percent to 15.3 percent of GDP, against a range of -1.1 percent to 16.2 percent of GDP, suggesting limited change in the model-based liquidity gap. On balance, the latest readings suggest more limited liquidity space and warrant closer monitoring of reserve buffers and external financing needs.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 40.0, with a moderate status and a vulnerability rank of 34 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is moderate vulnerability, placing the country between the low-risk group and the most pressured cases in the sample. The component scores are 50.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 100.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, which are outside the scope of this dashboard. The composite result is useful for cross-country screening, but the component indicators remain essential for identifying the source of vulnerability.

From a surveillance perspective, the ranking should be treated as a screening signal rather than a substitute for country-specific judgment. The component indicators remain more informative than the headline score: import cover captures reserve liquidity against trade needs, the short-term-debt ratio captures rollover exposure, and the baseline liquidity gap indicates whether usable reserves exceed the model's near-term liquidity requirement.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. Accordingly, the note should be read as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign-risk or balance-of-payments assessment.

# Mongolia

## Summary Assessment

Mongolia is an Upper middle income economy in East Asia & Pacific, with the latest observation in 2024. The external-liquidity screen records a vulnerability score of 40.0 and a moderate status. The latest reserve position is US$5.5 billion, providing 4.0 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 2.35x. The current account shows a deficit of 10.4% of GDP, equal to -US$2.5 billion. The model-estimated baseline liquidity gap is 14.6% of GDP, or US$3.5 billion. The assessment is driven by the interaction of reserve coverage, short-term external debt exposure, and the current-account position.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$5.5 billion, compared with annual imports of US$16.6 billion and GDP of US$23.8 billion. Import cover stood at 4.0 months. Under the scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 50.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$2.3 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 2.35x. The short-term-debt coverage component also does not add to the composite risk score in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$3.5 billion, or 14.6% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 10.4% of GDP, equivalent to -US$2.5 billion. The deficit is the main flow-side pressure in the dashboard, indicating a need for net external financing on the current-account measure. The current-account component carries a risk score of 100.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$5.5 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$2.5 billion. Imports of US$16.6 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$2.3 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. The latest reading should therefore be interpreted as a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage.

## Trend Interpretation

Over 2017-2024, the available indicators show how the liquidity position has evolved across reserve coverage, external flows, and the model-based liquidity gap. Import cover declined from 5.6 to 4.0 months, compared with a range of 3.3-7.4 months over the period. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt increased from 0.9x to 2.4x, while the observed range was 0.9x-3.5x. The current-account balance was broadly unchanged from -10.1 percent to -10.4 percent of GDP, with observations ranging from -15.2 percent to 0.6 percent of GDP. This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap narrowed from 25.5 percent to 14.6 percent of GDP, against a range of -5.7 percent to 28.2 percent of GDP, indicating some easing in the model-based liquidity gap. On balance, the latest readings suggest more limited liquidity space and warrant closer monitoring of reserve buffers and external financing needs.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 40.0, with a moderate status and a vulnerability rank of 33 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is moderate vulnerability, placing the country between the low-risk group and the most pressured cases in the sample. The component scores are 50.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 100.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, which are outside the scope of this dashboard. The composite result is useful for cross-country screening, but the component indicators remain essential for identifying the source of vulnerability.

From a surveillance perspective, the ranking should be treated as a screening signal rather than a substitute for country-specific judgment. The component indicators remain more informative than the headline score: import cover captures reserve liquidity against trade needs, the short-term-debt ratio captures rollover exposure, and the baseline liquidity gap indicates whether usable reserves exceed the model's near-term liquidity requirement.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. Accordingly, the note should be read as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign-risk or balance-of-payments assessment.

<!-- COUNTRY: NPL | Nepal -->

# Nepal

## Summary Assessment

Nepal is a Lower middle income economy in South Asia, with the latest observation in 2024. The external-liquidity screen records a vulnerability score of 0.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$15.8 billion, providing 13.5 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 37.47x. The current account shows a surplus of 3.9% of GDP, equal to US$1.7 billion. The model-estimated baseline liquidity gap is -27.7% of GDP, or -US$11.9 billion. The assessment is driven by the interaction of reserve coverage, short-term external debt exposure, and the current-account position.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$15.8 billion, compared with annual imports of US$14.1 billion and GDP of US$42.9 billion. Import cover stood at 13.5 months. Under the scoring framework, this import-cover position does not add to the composite risk score. Short-term external debt was US$422.9 million, and reserves covered that stock by 37.47x. The short-term-debt coverage component also does not add to the composite risk score in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at -US$11.9 billion, or -27.7% of GDP, which indicates a reserve cushion under the packet's baseline liquidity metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a surplus of 3.9% of GDP, equivalent to US$1.7 billion. The surplus is supportive for external liquidity, as it provides a flow-side source of foreign exchange rather than an immediate financing need on this measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$15.8 billion, while the current-account balance is US$1.7 billion. Imports of US$14.1 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$422.9 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The negative baseline gap indicates that reserves exceed the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. The latest reading should therefore be interpreted as a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage.

## Trend Interpretation

Over 2017-2024, the available indicators show how the liquidity position has evolved across reserve coverage, external flows, and the model-based liquidity gap. Import cover increased from 10.6 to 13.5 months, compared with a range of 6.4-13.5 months over the period. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt increased from 22.4x to 37.5x, while the observed range was 21.6x-37.5x. The current-account balance improved from -3.6 percent to 3.9 percent of GDP, with observations ranging from -14.5 percent to 3.9 percent of GDP. This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap narrowed from -18.3 percent to -27.7 percent of GDP, against a range of -27.7 percent to -1.2 percent of GDP, indicating some easing in the model-based liquidity gap. On balance, the latest position remains supported by reserve buffers, although the direction of individual indicators should continue to be monitored.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 0.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 84 out of 99 countries. The low classification indicates that the composite screen does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, which are outside the scope of this dashboard. The composite result is useful for cross-country screening, but the component indicators remain essential for identifying the source of vulnerability.

From a surveillance perspective, the ranking should be treated as a screening signal rather than a substitute for country-specific judgment. The component indicators remain more informative than the headline score: import cover captures reserve liquidity against trade needs, the short-term-debt ratio captures rollover exposure, and the baseline liquidity gap indicates whether usable reserves exceed the model's near-term liquidity requirement.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. Accordingly, the note should be read as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign-risk or balance-of-payments assessment.

The latest reading should therefore be interpreted as a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage.

## Trend Interpretation

Over 2015-2022, the available indicators show how the liquidity position has evolved across reserve coverage, external flows, and the model-based liquidity gap. Import cover increased from 15.7 to 20.2 months, compared with a range of 15.7-23.5 months over the period. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt increased from 7.6x to 8.9x, while the observed range was 7.4x-9.7x. The current-account balance improved from 5.0 percent to 10.4 percent of GDP, with observations ranging from 1.9 percent to 10.4 percent of GDP. This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap was broadly unchanged from -18.3 percent to -18.8 percent of GDP, against a range of -30.7 percent to -18.3 percent of GDP, suggesting limited change in the model-based liquidity gap. On balance, the latest position remains supported by reserve buffers, although the direction of individual indicators should continue to be monitored.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 0.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 95 out of 99 countries. The low classification indicates that the composite screen does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, which are outside the scope of this dashboard. The composite result is useful for cross-country screening, but the component indicators remain essential for identifying the source of vulnerability.

From a surveillance perspective, the ranking should be treated as a screening signal rather than a substitute for country-specific judgment. The component indicators remain more informative than the headline score: import cover captures reserve liquidity against trade needs, the short-term-debt ratio captures rollover exposure, and the baseline liquidity gap indicates whether usable reserves exceed the model's near-term liquidity requirement.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2015 and ends in 2022; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The latest observation is 2022, so the public-data indicators are incomplete for assessing developments after that year. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. Accordingly, the note should be read as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign-risk or balance-of-payments assessment.

# Rwanda

## Summary Assessment

Rwanda is a Low income economy in Sub-Saharan Africa, with the latest observation in 2024. The external-liquidity screen records a vulnerability score of 20.0 and a low status. The latest reserve position is US$2.4 billion, providing 5.2 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 4.80x. The current account shows a deficit of 12.7% of GDP, equal to -US$1.8 billion. The model-estimated baseline liquidity gap is 9.1% of GDP, or US$1.3 billion. The assessment is driven by the interaction of reserve coverage, short-term external debt exposure, and the current-account position.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$2.4 billion, compared with annual imports of US$5.6 billion and GDP of US$14.3 billion. Import cover stood at 5.2 months. Under the scoring framework, this import-cover position does not add to the composite risk score. Short-term external debt was US$500.8 million, and reserves covered that stock by 4.80x. The short-term-debt coverage component also does not add to the composite risk score in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$1.3 billion, or 9.1% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 12.7% of GDP, equivalent to -US$1.8 billion. The deficit is the main flow-side pressure in the dashboard, indicating a need for net external financing on the current-account measure. The current-account component carries a risk score of 100.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$2.4 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$1.8 billion. Imports of US$5.6 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$500.8 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. The latest reading should therefore be interpreted as a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage.

## Trend Interpretation

Over 2017-2024, the available indicators show how the liquidity position has evolved across reserve coverage, external flows, and the model-based liquidity gap. Import cover increased from 4.6 to 5.2 months, compared with a range of 3.8-5.8 months over the period. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt increased from 3.9x to 4.8x, while the observed range was 3.8x-4.8x. The current-account balance weakened from -9.5 percent to -12.7 percent of GDP, with observations ranging from -12.7 percent to -9.4 percent of GDP. This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap increased from 8.3 percent to 9.1 percent of GDP, against a range of 6.5 percent to 11.8 percent of GDP, leaving the liquidity cushion less comfortable than at the start of the period. On balance, the latest readings suggest more limited liquidity space and warrant closer monitoring of reserve buffers and external financing needs.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 20.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 42 out of 99 countries. The low classification indicates that the composite screen does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 100.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, which are outside the scope of this dashboard. The composite result is useful for cross-country screening, but the component indicators remain essential for identifying the source of vulnerability.

From a surveillance perspective, the ranking should be treated as a screening signal rather than a substitute for country-specific judgment. The component indicators remain more informative than the headline score: import cover captures reserve liquidity against trade needs, the short-term-debt ratio captures rollover exposure, and the baseline liquidity gap indicates whether usable reserves exceed the model's near-term liquidity requirement.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. Accordingly, the note should be read as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign-risk or balance-of-payments assessment.

The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. Accordingly, the note should be read as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign-risk or balance-of-payments assessment.

<!-- COUNTRY: SWZ | Eswatini -->

# Eswatini

## Summary Assessment

Eswatini is a Lower middle income economy in Sub-Saharan Africa, with the latest observation in 2024. The external-liquidity screen records a vulnerability score of 40.0 and a moderate status. The latest reserve position is US$536.6 million, providing 2.5 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 7.68x. The current account shows a surplus of 1.6% of GDP, equal to US$78.3 million. The model-estimated baseline liquidity gap is 1.4% of GDP, or US$69.9 million. The assessment is driven by the interaction of reserve coverage, short-term external debt exposure, and the current-account position.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$536.6 million, compared with annual imports of US$2.6 billion and GDP of US$4.9 billion. Import cover stood at 2.5 months. Under the scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 100.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$69.9 million, and reserves covered that stock by 7.68x. The short-term-debt coverage component also does not add to the composite risk score in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$69.9 million, or 1.4% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a surplus of 1.6% of GDP, equivalent to US$78.3 million. The surplus is supportive for external liquidity, as it provides a flow-side source of foreign exchange rather than an immediate financing need on this measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$536.6 million, while the current-account balance is US$78.3 million. Imports of US$2.6 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$69.9 million adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. The latest reading should therefore be interpreted as a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage.

## Trend Interpretation

Over 2017-2024, the available indicators show how the liquidity position has evolved across reserve coverage, external flows, and the model-based liquidity gap. Import cover declined from 3.5 to 2.5 months, compared with a range of 2.4-3.9 months over the period. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt increased from 3.1x to 7.7x, while the observed range was 3.1x-37.8x. The current-account balance weakened from 6.1 percent to 1.6 percent of GDP, with observations ranging from -3.0 percent to 6.6 percent of GDP. This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap narrowed from 2.4 percent to 1.4 percent of GDP, against a range of -2.5 percent to 3.2 percent of GDP, indicating some easing in the model-based liquidity gap. On balance, the latest readings suggest more limited liquidity space and warrant closer monitoring of reserve buffers and external financing needs.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 40.0, with a moderate status and a vulnerability rank of 31 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is moderate vulnerability, placing the country between the low-risk group and the most pressured cases in the sample. The component scores are 100.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, which are outside the scope of this dashboard. The composite result is useful for cross-country screening, but the component indicators remain essential for identifying the source of vulnerability.

From a surveillance perspective, the ranking should be treated as a screening signal rather than a substitute for country-specific judgment. The component indicators remain more informative than the headline score: import cover captures reserve liquidity against trade needs, the short-term-debt ratio captures rollover exposure, and the baseline liquidity gap indicates whether usable reserves exceed the model's near-term liquidity requirement.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. Accordingly, the note should be read as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign-risk or balance-of-payments assessment.

Imports of US$383.5 million create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$0 adds a stock-related liquidity test. The negative baseline gap indicates that reserves exceed the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. The latest reading should therefore be interpreted as a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage.

## Trend Interpretation

Over 2016-2023, the available indicators show how the liquidity position has evolved across reserve coverage, external flows, and the model-based liquidity gap. Import cover increased from 8.0 to 12.4 months, compared with a range of 7.9-14.5 months over the period. The current-account balance was broadly unchanged from -8.2 percent to -7.9 percent of GDP, with observations ranging from -9.5 percent to -6.6 percent of GDP. This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap narrowed from -17.9 percent to -42.9 percent of GDP, against a range of -45.8 percent to -17.9 percent of GDP, indicating some easing in the model-based liquidity gap. On balance, the trend should be interpreted through material movements and remaining buffers, not through small year-to-year changes in any single indicator.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 20.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 56 out of 99 countries. The low classification indicates that the composite screen does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 100.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, which are outside the scope of this dashboard. The composite result is useful for cross-country screening, but the component indicators remain essential for identifying the source of vulnerability.

From a surveillance perspective, the ranking should be treated as a screening signal rather than a substitute for country-specific judgment. The component indicators remain more informative than the headline score: import cover captures reserve liquidity against trade needs, the short-term-debt ratio captures rollover exposure, and the baseline liquidity gap indicates whether usable reserves exceed the model's near-term liquidity requirement.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2016 and ends in 2023; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The latest observation is 2023, so the public-data indicators are incomplete for assessing developments after that year. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. Accordingly, the note should be read as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign-risk or balance-of-payments assessment.

# Tunisia

## Summary Assessment

Tunisia is a Lower middle income economy in Middle East, North Africa, Afghanistan & Pakistan, with the latest observation in 2024. The external-liquidity screen records a vulnerability score of 60.0 and a high status. The latest reserve position is US$9.3 billion, providing 3.9 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 0.59x. The current account shows a deficit of 1.5% of GDP, equal to -US$775.3 million. The model-estimated baseline liquidity gap is 28.4% of GDP, or US$14.6 billion. The assessment is driven by the interaction of reserve coverage, short-term external debt exposure, and the current-account position.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$9.3 billion, compared with annual imports of US$28.9 billion and GDP of US$51.3 billion. Import cover stood at 3.9 months. Under the scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 50.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$15.9 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 0.59x. The short-term-debt coverage component has a risk score of 100.0, so debt-service concentration at the short end remains a relevant liquidity issue in the dashboard. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$14.6 billion, or 28.4% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a deficit of 1.5% of GDP, equivalent to -US$775.3 million. The deficit is the main flow-side pressure in the dashboard, indicating a need for net external financing on the current-account measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$9.3 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$775.3 million. Imports of US$28.9 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$15.9 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. The latest reading should therefore be interpreted as a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage.

## Trend Interpretation

Over 2017-2024, the available indicators show how the liquidity position has evolved across reserve coverage, external flows, and the model-based liquidity gap. Import cover increased from 3.2 to 3.9 months, compared with a range of 2.7-6.0 months over the period. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt declined from 0.8x to 0.6x, while the observed range was 0.6x-0.9x. The current-account balance improved from -10.0 percent to -1.5 percent of GDP, with observations ranging from -10.8 percent to -1.5 percent of GDP. This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap was broadly unchanged from 26.7 percent to 28.4 percent of GDP, against a range of 19.5 percent to 37.6 percent of GDP, suggesting limited change in the model-based liquidity gap. On balance, the latest readings suggest more limited liquidity space and warrant closer monitoring of reserve buffers and external financing needs.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 60.0, with a high status and a vulnerability rank of 15 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is high vulnerability, so the country sits in the more pressured segment of the sample on the packet's composite measure. The component scores are 50.0 for import-cover risk, 100.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, which are outside the scope of this dashboard. The composite result is useful for cross-country screening, but the component indicators remain essential for identifying the source of vulnerability.

From a surveillance perspective, the ranking should be treated as a screening signal rather than a substitute for country-specific judgment. The component indicators remain more informative than the headline score: import cover captures reserve liquidity against trade needs, the short-term-debt ratio captures rollover exposure, and the baseline liquidity gap indicates whether usable reserves exceed the model's near-term liquidity requirement.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. Accordingly, the note should be read as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign-risk or balance-of-payments assessment.

<!-- COUNTRY: VNM | Viet Nam -->

# Viet Nam

## Summary Assessment

Viet Nam is a Lower middle income economy in East Asia & Pacific, with the latest observation in 2024. The external-liquidity screen records a vulnerability score of 40.0 and a moderate status. The latest reserve position is US$83.1 billion, providing 2.5 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 2.24x. The current account shows a surplus of 6.3% of GDP, equal to US$30.2 billion. The model-estimated baseline liquidity gap is 7.8% of GDP, or US$37.1 billion. The assessment is driven by the interaction of reserve coverage, short-term external debt exposure, and the current-account position.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$83.1 billion, compared with annual imports of US$398.8 billion and GDP of US$476.4 billion. Import cover stood at 2.5 months. Under the scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 100.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$37.1 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 2.24x. The short-term-debt coverage component also does not add to the composite risk score in the latest observation. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$37.1 billion, or 7.8% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a surplus of 6.3% of GDP, equivalent to US$30.2 billion. The surplus is supportive for external liquidity, as it provides a flow-side source of foreign exchange rather than an immediate financing need on this measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$83.1 billion, while the current-account balance is US$30.2 billion. Imports of US$398.8 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$37.1 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. The latest reading should therefore be interpreted as a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage.

## Trend Interpretation

Over 2017-2024, the available indicators show how the liquidity position has evolved across reserve coverage, external flows, and the model-based liquidity gap. Import cover remained broadly stable from 2.6 to 2.5 months, compared with a range of 2.5-4.2 months over the period. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt remained broadly stable from 2.2x to 2.2x, while the observed range was 2.2x-3.6x. The current-account balance improved from -0.6 percent to 6.3 percent of GDP, with observations ranging from -1.3 percent to 6.3 percent of GDP. This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap was broadly unchanged from 8.4 percent to 7.8 percent of GDP, against a range of 0.0 percent to 9.2 percent of GDP, suggesting limited change in the model-based liquidity gap. On balance, the latest readings suggest more limited liquidity space and warrant closer monitoring of reserve buffers and external financing needs.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 40.0, with a moderate status and a vulnerability rank of 37 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is moderate vulnerability, placing the country between the low-risk group and the most pressured cases in the sample. The component scores are 100.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, which are outside the scope of this dashboard. The composite result is useful for cross-country screening, but the component indicators remain essential for identifying the source of vulnerability.

From a surveillance perspective, the ranking should be treated as a screening signal rather than a substitute for country-specific judgment. The component indicators remain more informative than the headline score: import cover captures reserve liquidity against trade needs, the short-term-debt ratio captures rollover exposure, and the baseline liquidity gap indicates whether usable reserves exceed the model's near-term liquidity requirement.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. Accordingly, the note should be read as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign-risk or balance-of-payments assessment.

## External Financing Pressure

This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap narrowed from 0.9 percent to -31.9 percent of GDP, against a range of -45.2 percent to 0.9 percent of GDP, indicating some easing in the model-based liquidity gap. On balance, the latest position remains supported by reserve buffers, although the direction of individual indicators should continue to be monitored.

The deficit is the main flow-side pressure in the dashboard, indicating a need for net external financing on the current-account measure. The current-account component carries a risk score of 50.0, making it an important contributor to the overall dashboard reading. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$4.1 billion, while the current-account balance is -US$1.1 billion. Imports of US$7.0 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$2.8 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. The latest reading should therefore be interpreted as a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage.

## Trend Interpretation

Over 2017-2024, the available indicators show how the liquidity position has evolved across reserve coverage, external flows, and the model-based liquidity gap. Import cover increased from 2.6 to 7.0 months, compared with a range of 1.9-7.0 months over the period. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt declined from 2.3x to 1.5x, while the observed range was 0.9x-2.5x. The current-account balance weakened from -1.7 percent to -4.4 percent of GDP, with observations ranging from -4.4 percent to 11.9 percent of GDP. This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap increased from 5.2 percent to 6.2 percent of GDP, against a range of 2.4 percent to 13.2 percent of GDP, leaving the liquidity cushion less comfortable than at the start of the period. On balance, the latest readings suggest more limited liquidity space and warrant closer monitoring of reserve buffers and external financing needs.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 10.0, with a low status and a vulnerability rank of 60 out of 99 countries. The low classification indicates that the composite screen does not flag acute external-liquidity stress in the latest observation. The component scores are 0.0 for import-cover risk, 0.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 50.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, which are outside the scope of this dashboard. The composite result is useful for cross-country screening, but the component indicators remain essential for identifying the source of vulnerability.

From a surveillance perspective, the ranking should be treated as a screening signal rather than a substitute for country-specific judgment. The component indicators remain more informative than the headline score: import cover captures reserve liquidity against trade needs, the short-term-debt ratio captures rollover exposure, and the baseline liquidity gap indicates whether usable reserves exceed the model's near-term liquidity requirement.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions. It also does not provide political, credit-rating, IMF-program, market-access, debt-event, reform-plan, or policy-measure information. Accordingly, the note should be read as a dashboard surveillance input rather than a full sovereign-risk or balance-of-payments assessment.

# Zimbabwe

## Summary Assessment

Zimbabwe is a Lower middle income economy in Sub-Saharan Africa, with the latest observation in 2024. The external-liquidity screen records a vulnerability score of 80.0 and a high status. The latest reserve position is US$485.0 million, providing 0.6 months of import cover and covering short-term external debt by 0.12x. The current account shows a surplus of 1.2% of GDP, equal to US$501.2 million. The model-estimated baseline liquidity gap is 9.6% of GDP, or US$4.0 billion. The assessment is driven by the interaction of reserve coverage, short-term external debt exposure, and the current-account position.

## Reserve Adequacy

In 2024, reported reserves were US$485.0 million, compared with annual imports of US$9.7 billion and GDP of US$41.5 billion. Import cover stood at 0.6 months. Under the scoring framework, the import-cover position carries a risk score of 100.0, indicating that the stock of reserves is not fully comfortable against the import bill. Short-term external debt was US$4.0 billion, and reserves covered that stock by 0.12x. The short-term-debt coverage component has a risk score of 100.0, so debt-service concentration at the short end remains a relevant liquidity issue in the dashboard. The baseline liquidity gap is reported at US$4.0 billion, or 9.6% of GDP, which indicates a baseline liquidity shortfall under the packet's metric. Taken together, the reserve stock should be interpreted not only against imports, but also against short-term external debt and the current-account position included in the packet.

## External Financing Pressure

The current account recorded a surplus of 1.2% of GDP, equivalent to US$501.2 million. The surplus is supportive for external liquidity, as it provides a flow-side source of foreign exchange rather than an immediate financing need on this measure. The current-account component contributes no risk to the vulnerability score. The comparison between the current-account balance and the reserve stock matters because reserves are US$485.0 million, while the current-account balance is US$501.2 million. Imports of US$9.7 billion create a recurring foreign-exchange need, and short-term external debt of US$4.0 billion adds a stock-related liquidity test. The positive baseline gap indicates that reserves fall short of the packet's combined baseline liquidity requirement. The latest reading should therefore be interpreted as a combined assessment of external flow pressure, import-payment capacity, and short-maturity debt coverage.

## Trend Interpretation

Over 2017-2024, the available indicators show how the liquidity position has evolved across reserve coverage, external flows, and the model-based liquidity gap. Import cover increased from 0.2 to 0.6 months, compared with a range of 0.1-1.0 months over the period. Reserve coverage of short-term external debt remained broadly stable from 0.1x to 0.1x, while the observed range was 0.0x-0.2x. The current-account balance improved from -0.5 percent to 1.2 percent of GDP, with observations ranging from -4.0 percent to 2.8 percent of GDP. This is the main flow-side counterpart to the reserve indicators. The baseline liquidity gap increased from 7.8 percent to 9.6 percent of GDP, against a range of 7.8 percent to 14.0 percent of GDP, leaving the liquidity cushion less comfortable than at the start of the period. On balance, the latest readings suggest more limited liquidity space and warrant closer monitoring of reserve buffers and external financing needs.

## Relative Vulnerability

The latest vulnerability score is 80.0, with a high status and a vulnerability rank of 5 out of 99 countries. The dashboard classification is high vulnerability, so the country sits in the more pressured segment of the sample on the packet's composite measure. The component scores are 100.0 for import-cover risk, 100.0 for short-term-debt coverage risk, and 0.0 for current-account risk. The ranking should be read as a relative dashboard position, not as a statement about market access, credit ratings, official financing, or policy commitments, which are outside the scope of this dashboard. The composite result is useful for cross-country screening, but the component indicators remain essential for identifying the source of vulnerability.

From a surveillance perspective, the ranking should be treated as a screening signal rather than a substitute for country-specific judgment. The component indicators remain more informative than the headline score: import cover captures reserve liquidity against trade needs, the short-term-debt ratio captures rollover exposure, and the baseline liquidity gap indicates whether usable reserves exceed the model's near-term liquidity requirement.

## Data Caveats

The recent-history field begins in 2017 and ends in 2024; it supports a limited indicator trend, but it does not provide monthly dynamics or intra-year stress episodes. The underlying dataset does not include exchange-rate regime details, reserve currency composition, usable-versus-gross reserve adjustments, amortization schedules beyond short-term external debt, contingent liabilities, remittance flows, capital-account details, or official financing assumptions.
